Lecture 1: Introduction and Overview

Lecture Overview

  1. Introductory Material:
    • Course, sections and waitlist.
    • The course: course objectives, logistics, assignments/grading, material and outline, etc.

  2. A quick demo of two one use cases that databases enable.

  4. Preparing for homework 1.

Introduction¶

Waitlist and Section¶

Waitlist¶

  • The administrative staff of the Dept. of Computer Science directly manages enrollment in the sections and the wait lists.
    • COMS W4111 is ALWAYS oversubscribed and has huge wait lists.
    • The course is a requirement for several majors and tracks.
    • There are complex rules about prioritizing students for course enrollment, and also which students can take the hybrid section.
  • The limiting factor on the standard, in-person lecture enrollment is the size of the room. We have to comply with university, city and state fire and safety codes.
  • Schools and departments have different rules about counting hybrid (H) sections for major and degree credits.
  • Summary: Contact the Dept. of Computer Science (Cynthia Meekins, cynthia@cs.columbia.edu) if you want to discuss the waitlist or enrollment in sections. You must work with the CS department if you have special circumstances for waitlist prioritization.

Sections¶

  • There are three identical sections for this course:
    1. 002: A conventional, in-person lecture format.
    2. H02: A "hybrid" section, in which students can watch the lecture live or access a recording.
    3. V02: Columbia Video Network.
  • I have authorized all students to have access to the videos.
    • H02 section students can attend lectures if there are open seats. (There will be. Students do not come to class).
    • You will find videos on CourseWorks:
      • The main folder is https://courseworks2.columbia.edu/courses/95783/external_tools/12497.
      • There will be a sub-folder for recorded office hours, recitations, etc.
  • The coursework, exams, assignments, TAs, ... are identical for all sections.
  • Note:
    • We cannot accommodate all students who want to take W4111 due to room size constraints.
    • If you join this in-person section, there is another student that I had to turn away and who cannot take the class.
    • Every time I come to a lecture and see empty seats,
      • I think of all the students who wanted to attend but we did not accommodate.
      • I also think of my commute time to get here, deferring other commitments to be here in person, ...
    • This makes me cranky. (Note: I am pretty cranky in general).
    • Students who do not attend lectures will come to office hours and ask questions on topics that I covered in lecture. Students come to office hours and ask questions that I answered in class.
    • This makes crankier.
    • There are several things I have considered to encourage in person attendance:
      • Not allowing non-hybrid, non-CVN students to have access to the video recordings.
      • Attendance extra-credit, attendance quizzes, etc.
    • You will still not attend lectures, might watch videos, ask redundant questions in office hours and on Piazza, etc. and there is nothing I can do about it.
    • So, I have decided to just be cranky. The key to happiness in life is lowered expectations.

The Course¶

Description¶

From the new/pending Columbia University Bulletin

"Prerequisites: COMS W3134, COMS W3136, or COMS W3137; or the instructor's permission.

The course covers what a database system is, how to design databases effectively and in a principled manner, how to query databases, and how to develop applications using databases: entity-relationship modeling, logical design of relational databases, relational algebra, SQL, database application development, database security, and an overview of query optimization and transaction processing. Additional topics generally include NoSQL, graph, object-relational, and cloud databases, as well as data preparation and cleaning of real-world data. The course offers both programming and non-programming paths for homework and projects, to accommodate students with different programming skills and backgrounds." (NOTE: Emphasis Added)

DFF NOTES:

  • COMS W3134, COMS W3136, or COMS W3137 are data structures classes. All of these courses require extensive programming, in Java.
  • Programming in/for this class:
    • There will be a "non-programming" option, which we will discuss below.
    • For students who want to take the programming track, we will use Python. I will provide motivation below.
    • A course in data structures is helpful for this section of W4111 but not essential.
      We will help you with any data structures knowledge you lack.

To Program or not to Program, That is the Question¶

From the department's new guidance for the course:

"To accommodate the diverse backgrounds of the students who take this class, all sections of the class should include a non-programming option for projects and assignments. We (NOTE: Does not include me) have successfully offered such an option in some sections of the class for many years: students can either program a web application to interface with a DB of their own design, or alternatively follow the non-programming option and come up with a quantifiably more detailed DB design/data.

What does not constitute programming?

  • Writing queries for a relational, graph, document, or other data management system does not constitute programming and can be expected of all students.

What constitutes programming?

  • Reading more than a handful (1-5) lines of code
  • Writing Python code that is not directly required to write a query."



Why I previous only did a programming track:

  • Any non-trivial use of data and databases in any field requires programming. This is not Star Trek. You cannot shout, "Computer! Analyze ..."
  • You might think, "But, I do not want to program. I want to go into financial services, private equity, ... I can just use tools like Pandas."
  • "Wes McKinney started working on pandas in 2008 while at AQR Capital Management out of the need for a high performance, flexible tool to perform quantitative analysis on financial data."https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pandas_(software) Even domain specific use of databases eventually requires some programming.
  • Students in previous semester, including non-CS, eventually found the programming projects very helpful and impressed people during internship/job interviews.
  • The workload will be the same but the tasks different between the two tracks.

Assignments, Exams and Grades¶

  • Point value of assignments and exams
    • 50%: Homework assignments: Approximately one HW every two weeks, for a total of 5.
    • Exams:
      • 20% of grade is midterm exam score.
      • 30% is final exam score.
    • A perfect sore of 100 points is an A. There are opportunities to get extra-credit.
  • Non-programming track:
    • Homework:
      • Questions requiring written answers and diagrams.
      • Implement/execution/test of various database operations required to solve a use case/question.
      • Format will be a iPython/Jupyter Notebook.
    • Exams:
      • Classic university exams.
      • Closed book, in lecture room, written in blue books, etc.
  • Programming track:
    • Homework:
      • Will be a set of programming projects that build on prior projects.
      • We will build key elements of a simple application.
    • Exams:
      • All exams are take home exams.
      • Students can access on-line material, textbooks, etc. to complete exams.
      • Will be written answers to questions and some simple code sections.
    • Submission format will be iPython/Jupyter Notebook.
  • NOTE:
    • We will pilot a new system, EdSTEM, which I hope will simplify the software environments.
    • I need to do some setup and testing.
  • Grading:
    • Based on total points in the range 0-100.
    • Perfect score is an A. A+ requires extra credit.
    • Extra-credit: Class participation, contributions on Piazza and office hour participation, extra work on assignments, etc. earn extra-credit points.
  • Late submission
    • You have a total of 5 grace days to apply for all homeworks.
    • 1 minute past the due date counts for 1 day. 24 hours + 1 minute counts for two days.
    • You cannot use grace days for midterm, final exam or extra-credit assignments.
    • NOTE:
      • Respect for the individual is paramount.
      • We will always accommodate illness, family emergencies, etc.

About this Course¶

  • This course is foundational, and will teach you the core concepts in
    • Data modeling
    • Data model implementation; Data manipulation.
    • Different database models and database management systems.
    • Implementation of data centric applications and database management systems.
  • ANY non-trivial application
    • Requires a well-designed data model.
    • Implements a data model and manipulates data.
    • Uses a database management system.
  • Understanding databases and database management is core to the “hottest fields” in computer science, e.g.
    • Data science
    • Machine learning
    • Intelligent (Autonomous) systems
    • Internet-of-Things
    • Cybersecurity
    • Cloud Computing
  • Database, database application, etc. skills are increasingly central to many disciplines, including economics, IEOR, mechanical and electrical engineering, business and finance, ...
  • University courses on databases sometimes focus on theory and abstract concepts. This course will cover theory, but the emphasis will be on:
    • Practical, hands-on applications of databases.
    • Developing and understanding database centric applications (programming track).
    • Patterns and best practices.
  • Personal perspective
    • A large percent of my career has been spent figuring out or leading teams that figured out how to model, implement and manipulate data.
    • I have used the information in this class more than anything else I have learned.
    • This will likely be true for you. </span>

Course Objectives¶

  • Have fun, learn a lot and come to appreciate and enjoy some amazing technology.
  • Provide a foundation that allows you to succeed in future courses. This is an introduction to databases. The technology is crucial for
    • Advanced database classes
    • Machine learning
    • Big data, data analysis
    • Numerical, simulation and analytics in operations research, engineering, economics, finance, life sciences, etc.
  • Enable you to successfully apply the technology in your work and profession.


Have cool stuff to talk about on interviews and in your resumes.

  • Copy into Notebook when "done.
  • Debugging is very hard without a good IDE debugger.
I suggest you do the same and use PyCharm.

In lectures, I will provide links to on-line material providing definitions and optional information. I often use Wikipedia because it is a good starting point and has many references.

Course Outline¶

Module I: Foundational Concepts

  1. Introduction to databases, role in applications, type of DB applications and overall system software architecture.

  2. Information and data modeling and best practices, focusing on supporting application scenarios.

  3. Relational data model (theory), Relational Database Management Systems, Structured Query Language, data query and update scenarios.

  4. Extended topics in SQL and RDBMS (performance, security, constraints, triggers, connection management, etc).

Module II: Database Management System Implementation/Architecture

  1. Storage management, disk management, buffer management, indexes.

  2. Query processing and optimization: Query evaluation, query parsing and parse trees, operator implementation algorithms, query rewrite, query optimization techniques.

  3. Concurrency control and transaction management.

Module III: NoSQL Database Overview

  1. Overview, graph databases, key-value databases, document databases.

  2. Amazon S3, Amazon DynamoDB, Google Firebase/Cloud Firestore.

Module IV: Decision Support, Data Analysis

  1. Overview of schema denormalization, OLAP cubes, data analytics, machine learning.

Two One Quick Demos¶

Motivating Example 1¶

Web Application¶

  • You are all familiar with http://www.imdb.com.
IMDB Search Result
  • How does this page work?
  • That is a good question. This is a web application. A simple overview of the general web application architecture ...
Web Application Architecture
  • You can think of the processing like calling a method or function in programming.
  • The browser sends a network message that is effectively a function call. The message contains:
    • Method name.
    • Identifies the "object" with a URL.
    • Parameters (path parameters, query parameters, body).
  • Several pieces come together on the server to implement the method:
    • Route handler code implementing method (Python, Java, etc).
    • Static content that forms a document/document elements (HTML, CSS, images, etc).
    • Downloaded code that will process and interpret data in the browser (JavaScript).
    • Data in one or more databases.
Web Application Architecture
  • The non-programming track will focus on designing, modeling and implementing databases that support applications.
  • The programming track will build elements of a simple web application, which will include concepts from the non-programming track.

The Data and Database¶

  • The IMDB site uses a database and a database management system. Why?
  • I downloaded the data: https://www.imdb.com/interfaces/
  • The file data information looks like:
IMDB Files
  • The downloaded files information in a tab separated file format.

    "A tab-separated values (TSV) file is a simple text format for storing data in a tabular structure, e.g., database table or spreadsheet data,[1] and a way of exchanging information between databases.[2] Each record in the table is one line of the text file. Each field value of a record is separated from the next by a tab character. The TSV format is thus a type of the more general delimiter-separated values format.

    TSV is a simple file format that is widely supported, so it is often used in data exchange to move tabular data between different computer programs that support the format. For example, a TSV file might be used to transfer information from a database program to a spreadsheet."
  • How much data do we have?
    • The sum of the bytes of the 7 zipped formats is 830 MB.
    • The unzipped size of all TSV files is 4 GB.
    • That MAY seem like a lot of data, but these are tiny by most real database scenarios.
  • Well, both Excel and Pages can read TSV files. Let's open the files using those programs.
  • That usually does not work. The files are too big. You can try if you want. We will have to use something else.

Programs¶

  • There are programs and functions in the web applications.
  • Let's write some simple programs to look at the files to get a feel for what these programs and functions look like.

  • These are like spreadsheet/Excel/Pages/Google Sheets files.
  • There is a Python library, like in all languages, for reading these files. The following function simply opens the files and counts the number of rows.
In [9]:
import csv

def count_rows(fn, delimiter):
    # fn is the fully qualified path and file name.
    # Delimiter is what separates the columns in the "spreadsheet."
    
    result = 0
    print("FN = ", fn)
    
    # Standard approach to opening a text file.
    with open(fn, "r") as in_file:
        
        # Use the CSV Reader to simplify parsing the the text file.
        # Delimiter specifies what separates the columns.
        # There are several other options, including whether or not quotes characters have a role.
        csv_rdr = csv.DictReader(in_file, delimiter=delimiter, quoting=csv.QUOTE_NONE)
        
        # For each row in the file.
        for r in csv_rdr:
            try:
                if result == 0:
                    print("For file = ", fn)
                    print("The colums are: ", csv_rdr.fieldnames)
                    print("The first rows as a dict is: ", r)
                result = result + 1
            except Exception as e:
                print("Could not read row, Exception = ", e)
                print("r = ", r)
                print("row count = ", result)

    return result
  • Just for fun, let's see how much time it takes to read all of the files?
In [10]:
total_rows = 0

start_time = time.time()
for f in data_files:
    print("\n**********")
    result = count_rows(file_dir + "/" + f, delimiter="\t")
    total_rows += result
end_time = time.time()
elapsed_time = end_time-start_time

print("\n\nThe total number of rows is = ", total_rows)
print("Total time to do the reading is ...", elapsed_time)

In [8]:
def list_tables(db_name):
    
    sql = "show tables from " + db_name
    cur = conn.cursor()
    res1 = cur.execute(sql)
    res = cur.fetchall()
    
    print("The tables in " + db_name + " are :")
    for r in res:
        for k,v in r.items():
            print("\t", v)

In [23]:
def count_rows_sql(table_name):
    # fn is the fully qualified path and file name.
    # Delimiter is what separates the columns in the "spreadsheet."
    
    result = 0
    print("table_name", table_name)
    
    # Wizard stuff. Will cover later.
    cur = conn.cursor()
    
    sql = "describe " + table_name
    res1 = cur.execute(sql)
    rows = cur.fetchall()
    
    columns = []
    for r in rows:
        columns.append(r['Field'])
    print("Columns = ", columns)

    res2 = cur.execute("select count(*) as count from " + table_name)
    rows = cur.fetchall()
    count = rows[0]['count']
    conn.commit()
    
    print("There are count = ", count, "rows")
    return count

end_time = time.time()
elapsed_time = end_time-start_time

print("\n\nThe total number of rows is = ", total_rows)
print("Total time to do the reading is ...", elapsed_time)

  • So,
    • Using the database engine was about 30 times faster despite using a network connection.
    • The loaded data was slightly different from what is in the files.
      • This is common when loading data.
      • Figuring out how to map from kind of junky file data into structured DB data is part of using databases.
  • Again, this seems like a lot of data but by real DB standards, it is tiny.

Let's Query the Files/Databases¶

  • Let's trying doing some "queries."
  • Also, maybe using some framework other than CSV DictReader might help. Let's try Pandas.
In [25]:
#
# A simple function that returns a row about a person.
#
fn = "../../Data/IMDB/TSVFormat/name_basics.tsv"

def find_by_name(name):
    start_time = time.time()
    df = pd.read_csv(fn, sep='\t')
    th = df[df['primaryName'] == name]
    end_time = time.time()
    elapsed_time = end_time-start_time
    print("Time to find people named ", name, "was ", elapsed_time)
    return th

In [28]:
def find_by_name_sql(name):
    start_time = time.time()
    df = pd.read_sql("select * from name_basics_fast where primary_name='" + name + "'", db_cnx)
    end_time = time.time()
    elapsed_time = end_time-start_time
    print("Time to find people named ", name, "using a database was ... was ", elapsed_time)
    return df

  • Using the database is 3 orders of magnitude faster, in this case approximately 400x faster.
  • The relative benefit would increase exponentially.
    • Using the file is $O(n)$ for a simple lookup for $n$ rows.
    • The database performance is $Olog(n)$.
  • Let's look at a more complicated query.
    • Define a function co_artists(name)
    • Returns the information about people who were principles in a movie/show/... with name, and returns each principle only once.
  • We need to understand the structure of the files. A little snooping around on the web indicates that the logical relationships between the files is.
IMDB Data Model
  • The is a data model diagram, or more precisely an entity-relationship diagram.
  • Some core concepts we will learn in this course are:
    • The roles of entity-relationship (data) modeling in solving problems with data and databases.
    • Design patterns and best practices for modeling and defining data.
  • We can use the data model to do an initial design for co_artists(name).
  • The logic is something like ... ...
IMDB Search Result
  • So, let's do this the non-database way and write some functions to process the files.
  • The first part of the task is to read and load the data into memory. We need
    • name_basics.tsv
    • title_basics.tsv
    • title_principals.tsv
In [32]:
def load_csv_file(fn, delimiter="\t", quoting=csv.QUOTE_NONE):
    """
    Reads a CSV file and returns a list of dictionaries representing each row.

    :param fn: Fully qualified path to the file.
    :param delimiter: Field delimiter.
    :param quoting: Quoting value for strings.
    :return: Array of OrderedDict representing rows in file.
    """

    result = []

    with open(fn, "r") as in_file:

        # Create a DictReader with the options passed into this function.
        csv_rdr = csv.DictReader(in_file, delimiter=delimiter, quoting=quoting)
        for r in csv_rdr:
            result.append(dict(r))

    return result
In [33]:
start_time = time.time()

name_basics_tbl = load_csv_file("../../Data/IMDB/TSVFormat/name_basics.tsv")
load1_time = time.time() - start_time

title_basics_tbl = load_csv_file("../../Data/IMDB/TSVFormat/title_basics.tsv")
load2_time = time.time() - start_time
print("Loaded ", len(title_basics_tbl), 'title_basics rows at elapsed time ', load2_time, 'seconds.')

title_principals_tbl = load_csv_file("../../Data/IMDB/TSVFormat/title_principals.tsv")

  • Let's write a generic function that will find entries in a loaded TSV table by field and value.
In [48]:
def find_by_field(csv_table, field_name, value):

    result = []

    for r in csv_table:
        test_v = r.get(field_name, None)
        if test_v == value:
            result.append(r)

    return result
In [53]:
find_start_time = time.time()
per = find_by_field(name_basics_tbl, 'primaryName', 'Tom Hanks')
find_elapsed_time = time.time() - find_start_time
nconst = per[0].get('nconst', None)
print("Time needed to find the person", per[0].get('primaryName', None), "took ", find_elapsed_time, 'seconds')

  • Now, for each of of those titles, we have to find the co-principals (other people in the title).
IMDB Search Step 3
  • The following piece of code puts some of the pieces together ...
def t2():

    name = "Tom Hanks"
    print("Finding all co_principals for ", name)
    print("Loading names_basics table")

    start_total_time = time.time()

    load1_start_time = time.time()
    names_tbl = load_csv_file(tsv_data_dir + name_basics_fn)
    load1_end_time = time.time()
    print("Loaded ", len(names_tbl), "rows in ", load1_end_time-load1_start_time, "seconds.")

    print("Starting lookup by name.")
    lookup1_start_time = time.time()
    p = find_by_field(names_tbl, "primaryName", name)
    lookup1_end_time = time.time()

    print("names_basics lookup returned ", p[0])
    print("Lookup time was ", lookup1_end_time-lookup1_start_time)

    nconst = p[0].get("nconst", None)

    print("Starting load of titles_principles table.")
    load2_start_time = time.time()
    titles_principals_tbl = load_csv_file(tsv_data_dir + title_principals_fn)
    load2_end_time = time.time()
    print("Loaded ", len(titles_principals_tbl), "rows.")
    print("Load time = ", load2_end_time-load2_start_time)

    print("Starting lookup for titles for ", nconst)
    lookup2_start_time = time.time()
    all_titles = find_by_field(titles_principals_tbl, "nconst", nconst)
    lookup2_end_time = time.time()
    print("Look titles elapsed time = ", lookup2_end_time - lookup2_start_time)
    print("Lookup returned ", len(all_titles), "titles.")

    all_co_artists = []
    for tt in all_titles:
        tc = tt.get("tconst", None)
        print("Looking up nconsts for tconst = ", tc)
        lookup_t_start_time = time.time()
        current_co_artists = find_by_field(titles_principals_tbl, "tconst", tc)
        lookup_t_end_time = time.time()
        print("Found ", len(current_co_artists), "co artists.")
        print("Lookup time = ", lookup_t_end_time-lookup_t_start_time)
        all_co_artists.extend(current_co_artists)

    total_end_time = time.time()

    print("All lookups done, found ", len(all_co_artists), "co artists.")
    print("Total time = ", total_end_time-start_total_time)

Looking
  • There are 623 related titles, and finding the co-principals requires about 40 seconds/title.
  • So, this is going to run for (623*40)/3600 = about 7 hours.
  • The database seems to track 10 principals/titles, which means:
    • we would have to look up about 6200 nconst values in name_basics table to get the names from the IDs.
    • Each lookup takes about 1.5 minutes, which would be (1.5*6200)/60 = approx. 155 hours.
    • This is would take a while.
  • There are design changes I could make to my application that would make it more efficient, but the changes would be specific to this application's data model.
  • Instead of using operating system files and "normal languages," let's try a database engine and a query language.
  • I loaded the data into a set of database tables.
  • Let's find 'Tom Hanks.'
  • First we have to do the database equivalent of "open some files."
In [4]:

  • Let's build this out a little ... Let's get all of the titles.

    nconst = (select nconst from imdbnew.name_basics_fast where primary_name='Tom Hanks')

end_time = time.time()

print("I found the answer in", end_time-start_time, "seconds.")

I found the answer in 0.015991926193237305 seconds.
  • Why did it get a different aswer? There were duplicates.
  • The time went from 90 seconds to less than a second.
  • Now, let's find the co-principals in the form (principal nconst, role).
In [7]:
start_time = time.time()

ans = %sql select nconst, primary_name from name_basics_fast where nconst in \
            (select distinct nconst from title_principals_rel where tconst in \
                (select tconst from imdbnew.title_principals_rel where  \
						nconst = (select nconst from imdbnew.name_basics_fast where primary_name='Tom Hanks')))
                        
end_time = time.time()
ans

import csv
import time

tsv_data_dir = "../../../Data/IMDB/TSVFormat/"
name_basics_fn = "name_basics.tsv"
title_basics_fn = "title_basics.tsv"
title_principals_fn = "title_principals.tsv"


def load_csv_file(fn, delimiter="\t", quoting=csv.QUOTE_NONE):
    """
    Reads a CSV file and returns a list of dictionaries representing each row.

    :param fn: Fully qualified path to the file.
    :param delimiter: Field delimiter.
    :param quoting: Quoting value for strings.
    :return: Array of OrderedDict representing rows in file.
    """

    result = []

    with open(fn, "r") as in_file:

        # Create a DictReader with the options passed into this function.
        csv_rdr = csv.DictReader(in_file, delimiter=delimiter, quoting=quoting)
        for r in csv_rdr:
            result.append(dict(r))

    return result


def find_by_field(csv_table, field_name, value):

    result = []

    for r in csv_table:
        test_v = r.get(field_name, None)
        if test_v == value:
            result.append(r)

    return result


def find_person(name=None, id=None):

    fn = tsv_data_dir + name_basics_fn
    tbl = load_csv_file(fn)

    if name is None and id is None:
        raise ValueError("Must pass one non-null parameter.")

    if id is not None:
        field = 'nconst'
        v = id
    else:
        field = 'primaryName'
        v = name

    result = find_by_field(tbl, field, v)
    return result


def t2():

    name = "Tom Hanks"
    print("Finding all co_principals for ", name)
    print("Loading names_basics table")

    start_total_time = time.time()

    load1_start_time = time.time()
    names_tbl = load_csv_file(tsv_data_dir + name_basics_fn)
    load1_end_time = time.time()
    print("Loaded ", len(names_tbl), "rows in ", load1_end_time-load1_start_time, "seconds.")

    print("Starting lookup by name.")
    lookup1_start_time = time.time()
    p = find_by_field(names_tbl, "primaryName", name)
    lookup1_end_time = time.time()

    print("names_basics lookup returned ", p[0])
    print("Lookup time was ", lookup1_end_time-lookup1_start_time)

    nconst = p[0].get("nconst", None)

    print("Starting load of titles_principles table.")
    load2_start_time = time.time()
    titles_principals_tbl = load_csv_file(tsv_data_dir + title_principals_fn)
    load2_end_time = time.time()
    print("Loaded ", len(titles_principals_tbl), "rows.")
    print("Load time = ", load2_end_time-load2_start_time)

    print("Starting lookup for titles for ", nconst)
    lookup2_start_time = time.time()
    all_titles = find_by_field(titles_principals_tbl, "nconst", nconst)
    lookup2_end_time = time.time()
    print("Look titles elapsed time = ", lookup2_end_time - lookup2_start_time)
    print("Lookup returned ", len(all_titles), "titles.")

    all_co_artists = []
    for tt in all_titles:
        tc = tt.get("tconst", None)
        print("Looking up nconsts for tconst = ", tc)
        lookup_t_start_time = time.time()
        current_co_artists = find_by_field(titles_principals_tbl, "tconst", tc)
        lookup_t_end_time = time.time()
        print("Found ", len(current_co_artists), "co artists.")
        print("Lookup time = ", lookup_t_end_time-lookup_t_start_time)
        all_co_artists.extend(current_co_artists)

    total_end_time = time.time()

    print("All lookups done, found ", len(all_co_artists), "co artists.")
    print("Total time = ", total_end_time-start_total_time)

                        nconst = (select nconst from imdbnew.name_basics_fast where primary_name='Tom Hanks')))

Technical Topics¶

What Does the Textbook Have to Say?¶

Some Other Concept¶

Some Ways to Think about Data¶

Structured vs Unstructured¶
Structured vs Unstructured
Structured Data¶
  • High degree of organization.
  • All records for a given type have
    • The same fields.
    • The fields are strongly typed.
    • There are integrity constraints on values, e.g. NOT NULL.
  • The relational model is the canonical example.
    • The data is structured, which means you must define the structure $\Rightarrow$ there is a data definition language (DDL). A DDL is similar to defining a class in Java, C++ or Python.
    • Instances of data (rows, tuples) must match the DDL in the same way objects match the class definition.
    • The Relational Model has additional capabilities:
      • Integrity constraints, e.g. NOT NULL. Primary Key means that the $student\_id$ is NOT NULL and UNIQUE.
      • Referential Integrity: For every Grade row, there must be one row with a matching $student\_id$ in Students.
      • Indexes: There is an auxiliary data structure that speeds up locating students using name fields.

  • The data is very structured, tabular, etc.
Semi-structured Data¶
  • There are records with fields.
  • You can parse the data to extract records and fields into data structures.
  • Records may have some common fields, but any record may have any fields of any types.
  • Documents are the canonical example:
    • There are common structural concepts: headers, lists, footnotes, etc.
    • But different documents have different combinations, nesting, etc.
  • CouchDB is an example of a document database. It stores semi-structured JSON documents.

Unstructured¶
  • No explicit records or delimiters. No fields. No properties.
  • Just a sequence of characters/bytes that can be interpreted by some processing engine.
  • Images, audio files, etc. are examples.

Unstructured Data
Course Focus¶
  • We will focus on structured data and some on semi-structured.
  • Need to constrain the scope for a one semester class.
  • Structured data concepts are foundational and apply to semi-structured and unstructured data.
  • Unstructured data scenarios often require extracting or extending with structured data.

Data versus Metadata¶

Concept¶
  • Metadata is "data information that provides information about other data". Three distinct types of metadata exist:
    1. Descriptive metadata
    2. Structural metadata
    3. Administrative metadata.
  • Descriptive metadata describes a resource for purposes such as discovery and identification. It can include elements such as title, abstract, author, and keywords.
  • Structural metadata is metadata about containers of data and indicates how compound objects are put together, for example, how pages are ordered to form chapters. It describes the types, versions, relationships and other characteristics of digital materials.
  • Administrative metadata provides information to help manage a resource, such as when and how it was created, file type and other technical information, and who can access it.
  • We will cover metadata, especially while studying relational data.

Data Explosion¶
Data Explosion
  • The world's total, yearly data creation will reach 163 zettabytes in 2025.
  • DANGER: Math before caffeine.
  • Zettabyte is $1000^7 = 10^{21}.$
  • World's population is/will be approximately $10 * 10^{9} = 10^{10}$
  • Data per person in 2025 =

    \begin{equation} \frac{1.63 * 10^2 * 10^{21}}{10^{10}} = \frac{1.63 * 10^{23}}{10^{10}} = 1.63 * 10^{13} = 16.3*10^{12} = 16.3*10^{3}*10^{9} = 16,000\ GB \ per \ person, \ per \ year. \end{equation}
  • In 2025, "an average connected person anywhere in the world will interact with connected devices nearly 4,800 times per day – one interaction every 18 seconds." Forbes. Each interaction creates new data.

The Four Vs¶
Four Vs of Data

Some driving factors:

  • Volume:
    • File/instance size: videos, images, VR, ...
    • Sources: Internet-of-Things, events and monitoring, ...
  • Velocity:
    • Monitoring and events from everything.
    • Video and audio from everywhere.
  • Veracity:
    • Measurement error and approximation.
    • Incorrect configuration, entry, processing applications.
  • Variety:
    • Base types: several base models for structured data.
    • Every device emits its own event format.
    • Multiple encodings and formats of text (mail, web page, tweet) audio, video, ...

Or, Maybe Five Vs¶
Five Vs
  • Value: "It's like trying to find a needle in a haystack size pile of needles."

Big data landscape¶
Big Data Landscape

Note to Instructor: Cover in lecture 2. Skipped in lecture 1.

  • There are many, many, many different kinds of database management systems. The differences are:
    • Type of data: tables, documents, images, complex design models, ...
    • Use case optimizations: ready only/query, business transactions, realtime data sharing, ...
    • Deployment scenarios: mobile device, application embedded, high-performance cloud, ...
  • We will go through a few representative databases in this course.
DBMS Taxonomy
  • We will cover the DBMS implementation in Module II. That is, what is the architecture and implementation of the DBMS SW.
  • To understand DBMS models and usage, we will primarily focus on 4 database management systems:
    1. Relational (MySQL)
    2. Graph Database (Neo4J)
    3. Key/Value- data structure store (Redis)
    4. Document (CouchDB)
  • Motivation:
    • https://db-engines.com/en/ranking adoption and growth.
    • Diversity of base models covers core patterns in functionality and performance.
DBMS popularity broken down by database model
Popularity changes per category, August 2018
  • Why start with MySQL? Popular, open source and free.
DB-Engines Ranking of Relational DBMS

In [1]:
from py2neo import Graph

graph = Graph(user="dbuser", password="dbuser")

from py2neo import Node

nicole = Node("Person", name="Nicole", age=24)
drew = Node("Person", name="Drew", age=20)

mtdew = Node("Drink", name="Mountain Dew", calories=9000)
cokezero = Node("Drink", name="Coke Zero", calories=0)

coke = Node("Manufacturer", name="Coca Cola")
pepsi = Node("Manufacturer", name="Pepsi")

graph.create(nicole | drew | mtdew | cokezero | coke | pepsi)
